              <div class="section">
               <div class="review" id="review_1195409992" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
                <link href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1195409992" itemprop="url"/>
                <a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28204661-dan-falk" title="Dan Falk">
                 <img alt="Dan Falk" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/users/1391093636p2/28204661.jpg"/>
                </a>
                <div class="left bodycol">
                 <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
                  <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1195409992?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">
                   Feb 09, 2015
                  </a>
                  <a class="user" href="/user/show/28204661-dan-falk" title="Dan Falk">
                   Dan Falk
                  </a>
                  rated it
                  <a class=" staticStars stars_3" title="liked it">
                   3 of 5 stars
                  </a>
                 </div>
                 <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1195409992" style="">
                   <span id="freeTextContainer14548066551011168809">
                    Nick Bostrom believes that computer superintelligence is one of the greatest threats facing our species, arguing the case in this comprehensive, scholarly work. Its not a light read: Bostrom, founding director of the Future of Humanity Institute at Oxford, has written a very thorough but also very dense treatise. (To be contrasted, for example, with
                    <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17286699.Our_Final_Invention__Artificial_Intelligence_and_the_End_of_the_Human_Era" title="Our Final Invention Artificial Intelligence and the End of the Human Era by James Barrat">
                     Our Final Invention: Artificial Intelligence and the End of the Human Era
                    </a>
                    by
                    <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6289858.James_Barrat" title="James Barrat">
                     James Barrat
                    </a>
                    , which explores similar issues.) Bostom has thought very
                   </span>
                   <span id="freeText14548066551011168809" style="display:none">
                    Nick Bostrom believes that computer superintelligence is one of the greatest threats facing our species, arguing the case in this comprehensive, scholarly work.  Its not a light read: Bostrom, founding director of the Future of Humanity Institute at Oxford, has written a very thorough but also very dense treatise.  (To be contrasted, for example, with
                    <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17286699.Our_Final_Invention__Artificial_Intelligence_and_the_End_of_the_Human_Era" title="Our Final Invention  Artificial Intelligence and the End of the Human Era by James Barrat">
                     Our Final Invention: Artificial Intelligence and the End of the Human Era
                    </a>
                    by
                    <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6289858.James_Barrat" title="James Barrat">
                     James Barrat
                    </a>
                    , which explores similar issues.)  Bostom has thought very deeply about existential threats in all their various forms.  He is, at times, almost convincing; perhaps he would be even more persuasive if the dangers he was describing didnt seem to lie so very, very far in the future.  For most of us, the things he describes are mere mirages at the edge of what we can foresee; for Bostrom, they are real, tangible, and dangerous.  There are also some awkward philosophical issues:  He talks about computers having goals and even moral beliefs  but the question of how they might come to feel anything at all is sidestepped.  It's good that some people -- a very small number of people, I suspect -- are worrying about the sort of rampaging-AI scenarios of the "Terminator"/"Skynet" variety.  I'm not planning to worry just yet.  (Note: We discuss Superintelligence at length in Episode 4 of our "BookLab" podcast.)
                   </span>
                   <a data-text-id="14548066551011168809" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">
                    ...more
                   </a>
                  </span>

              <div class="section">
               <div class="review" id="review_1130932253" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
                <link href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1130932253" itemprop="url"/>
                <a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1859963-suhrob" title="Suhrob">
                 <img alt="Suhrob" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/users/1319383031p2/1859963.jpg"/>
                </a>
                <div class="left bodycol">
                 <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
                  <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1130932253?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">
                   Dec 13, 2014
                  </a>
                  <a class="user" href="/user/show/1859963-suhrob" title="Suhrob">
                   Suhrob
                  </a>
                  rated it
                  <a class=" staticStars stars_4" title="really liked it">
                   4 of 5 stars
                  </a>
                 </div>
                 <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1130932253" style="">
                   <span id="freeTextContainer9763598867134618106">
                    Bostrom's introduction into the problematics of Artificial General Intelligence (AGI) is a very good first stop for newcomers to the topic. The material focuses mostly on existential risk factors and mitigation strategies. The book gives also some basic historical context and the barest minimum background on the more technical side of the AGI research, architecture etc.
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    Recommended for general audience as this is an essential topic and the books is overall very competent.
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    That being said... I was
                   </span>
                   <span id="freeText9763598867134618106" style="display:none">
                    Bostrom's introduction into the problematics of Artificial General Intelligence (AGI) is a very good first stop for newcomers to the topic. The material focuses mostly on existential risk factors and mitigation strategies. The book gives also some basic historical context and the barest minimum background on the more technical side of the AGI research, architecture etc.
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    Recommended for general audience as this is an essential topic and the books is overall very competent.
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    That being said... I was very, very disappointed by it...
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    For those that are already familiar with this area there is very little new here. It is a good, but not great summary of the risk/strategy aspects but is deeply lacking on all other aspects (decision theories/interaction with the objective function/Lob's obstacle etc.). Many of the most fascinating issues are completely omitted, I guess understandably given the main aim of the book, but still...
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    As a long time fan of Bostrom's writing this was also a surprisingly slog to read through. The most important parts are essentially re-telling of Yudkowsky's and Hanson's work. There is very little original Bostrom - and as example,his own Orthogonality thesis is essentially just stated, with the argumentation being very cursory and incomplete.
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    As said - I do recommend it generally, but can't say I'm not disappointed. It really should be 3 stars, but I want to encourage its wider reading just because of the import of the topic.
                   </span>
                   <a data-text-id="9763598867134618106" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">
                    ...more
                   </a>
                  </span>

             <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown">
              <div class="section">
               <div class="review" id="review_1161811349" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
                <link href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1161811349" itemprop="url"/>
                <a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1571969-billie-pritchett" title="Billie Pritchett">
                 <img alt="Billie Pritchett" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/users/1241942909p2/1571969.jpg"/>
                </a>
                <div class="left bodycol">
                 <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
                  <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1161811349?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">
                   Jan 13, 2015
                  </a>
                  <a class="user" href="/user/show/1571969-billie-pritchett" title="Billie Pritchett">
                   Billie Pritchett
                  </a>
                  rated it
                  <a class=" staticStars stars_3" title="liked it">
                   3 of 5 stars
                  </a>
                 </div>
                 <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1161811349" style="">
                   <span id="freeTextContainer10766691266800966599">
                    Gosh, it's really hard to review Nick Bostrom's
                    <em>
                     Superintelligence
                    </em>
                    . I imagine works like these tend to be pretty polarizing. Here's why. So Nick Bostrom is this really, super-duper smart guy who releases this book about what we ought to to do if we create some superintelligence. He defines a "superintelligence" quite generally as anything that's smarter than the human race now.
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    I don't want to knit-pick, really, and I'd prefer to go ahead and assume that he's correct. That is to say, he's correct
                   </span>
                   <span id="freeText10766691266800966599" style="display:none">
                    Gosh, it's really hard to review Nick Bostrom's
                    <em>
                     Superintelligence
                    </em>
                    . I imagine works like these tend to be pretty polarizing. Here's why. So Nick Bostrom is this really, super-duper smart guy who releases this book about what we ought to to do if we create some superintelligence. He defines a "superintelligence" quite generally as anything that's smarter than the human race now.
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    I don't want to knit-pick, really, and I'd prefer to go ahead and assume that he's correct. That is to say, he's correct about this:
                    <em>
                     If
                    </em>
                    we created some super-smart AI, it would introduce all sorts of problems and dangers, and we'd have to plan for these. Like, for example, the AI could get way out of hand and try to enslave us or something. Okay.
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    The operative word in Bostrom's book's thesis is
                    <em>
                     if
                    </em>
                    . Yes, we'd have to take a lot of precautions if we came up with some super-smart AI, but are we even near coming up with something like this? I have my doubts. First of all, because we don't have anything like an explanatory theory for what
                    <em>
                     intelligence
                    </em>
                    is. So at a deep explanatory level, that's just a mystery. So if we don't know what it is, and how to account for it, then we're sure as heck not going to be able to program an AI with it. There might be some sub-subdomains you can program, though. Like right now, there are AIs that know the optimal moves for checkers. And Deep Blue was a wicked smart chess player. Okay, fine. And there are dangers with those systems as well, I'm sure. And probably most dangerous of all are any AIs that have any interaction with nuclear weapons. At this point, you can insert your own Dr. Strangelove scenario. Something like a Strangelove AI would be something to worry about. But that, nor none of what I'm talking about is a superintelligence in the way Bostrom conceives of it.
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    It's not that Bostrom thinks that we have a superintelligence now, it's just that he thinks one is one the way. He's agnostic about when it'll come but he hedges his bets in that direction. But again, we don't understand intelligence, we can't program an AI with an intelligence right now, so what makes us think we'll ever arrive at this point?
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    If we get there, I'll eat crow. Till then, I'm going to hedge the other way, with the rest of the Vulgar.
                   </span>
                   <a data-text-id="10766691266800966599" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">
                    ...more
                   </a>
                  </span>
                 </div>

              <div class="section">
               <div class="review" id="review_1132788446" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
                <link href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1132788446" itemprop="url"/>
                <a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/34049748-jerrod-carter" title="Jerrod Carter">
                 <img alt="Jerrod Carter" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/users/1414499703p2/34049748.jpg"/>
                </a>
                <div class="left bodycol">
                 <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
                  <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1132788446?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">
                   Dec 20, 2014
                  </a>
                  <a class="user" href="/user/show/34049748-jerrod-carter" title="Jerrod Carter">
                   Jerrod Carter
                  </a>
                  rated it
                  <a class=" staticStars stars_2" title="it was ok">
                   2 of 5 stars
                  </a>
                  <span class="greyText">
                   
                  </span>
                  <a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/23877508-superintelligence" title="Audible Audio ">
                   review of another edition
                  </a>
                 </div>
                 <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1132788446" style="">
                   <span id="freeTextContainer7030755931828195421">
                    I was hoping this would have been more interesting. It was about the possible ways humanity might develop an artificial form of intelligence and what we could do in advance to keep that from becoming an existential threat in the case that it was able to improve its own intelligence so quickly that it would quickly outstrip us and either purposefully or accidentally take over the world and destroy us in the process.
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    This is a possibility, but the treatment of the material was so theoretical and bu
                   </span>
                   <span id="freeText7030755931828195421" style="display:none">
                    I was hoping this would have been more interesting. It was about the possible ways humanity might develop an artificial form of intelligence and what we could do in advance to keep that from becoming an existential threat in the case that it was able to improve its own intelligence so quickly that it would quickly outstrip us and either purposefully or accidentally take over the world and destroy us in the process.
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    This is a possibility, but the treatment of the material was so theoretical and built hypotheticals upon hypotheticals so deeply that it was hard to keep engaged.
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    Really, I think you'd be better served to watch the movie Bedazzled and pretend that the devil (played by the lovely Elizabeth Hurley) was a humankind created entity, and you'd more or less have the idea of the magnitude of the problem.
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    I do think he also completely discounted the possibility of a sociopath becoming involved in an AI project and malevolently trying to create a superintelligence that would take over the world, thinking that the sociopath him/herself would control the AI. That'd pretty much be end of game if the evil genius was able to succeed--life as we know it over.
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    Anyway, be prepared for arcane stuff if you pick up this one.
                   </span>
                   <a data-text-id="7030755931828195421" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">
                    ...more
                   </a>
                  </span>

             <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown">
              <div class="section">
               <div class="review" id="review_1306892059" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
                <link href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1306892059" itemprop="url"/>
                <a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11936064-djcb" title="djcb">
                 <img alt="djcb" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/users/1372499420p2/11936064.jpg"/>
                </a>
                <div class="left bodycol">
                 <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
                  <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1306892059?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">
                   Jun 20, 2015
                  </a>
                  <a class="user" href="/user/show/11936064-djcb" title="djcb">
                   djcb
                  </a>
                  rated it
                  <a class=" staticStars stars_4" title="really liked it">
                   4 of 5 stars
                  </a>
                 </div>
                 <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1306892059" style="">
                   <span id="freeTextContainer16794140598174079797">
                    A book about the coming (soon or in decades) super-intelligences, either by whole-brain emulation (with improvement), or through artificial intelligence (AI).
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    Similar in subject to
                    <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17286699.Our_Final_Invention__Artificial_Intelligence_and_the_End_of_the_Human_Era" title="Our Final Invention Artificial Intelligence and the End of the Human Era by James Barrat">
                     Our Final Invention: Artificial Intelligence and the End of the Human Era
                    </a>
                    , this book discusses the strategies for developing such a super-intelligence (and whether we should do so at all), the various possible consequences, and the control problem (ie., how can we control the super intelligence to not do something huma
                   </span>
                   <span id="freeText16794140598174079797" style="display:none">
                    A book about the coming (soon or in decades) super-intelligences, either by whole-brain emulation (with improvement), or through artificial intelligence (AI).
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    Similar in subject to
                    <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17286699.Our_Final_Invention__Artificial_Intelligence_and_the_End_of_the_Human_Era" title="Our Final Invention  Artificial Intelligence and the End of the Human Era by James Barrat">
                     Our Final Invention: Artificial Intelligence and the End of the Human Era
                    </a>
                    , this book discusses the strategies for developing such a super-intelligence (and whether we should do so at all), the various possible consequences, and the control problem (ie., how can we control the super intelligence to not do something humanity would not like). The book is not about the technical aspects of creating a super-intelligence (which are still nebulous at best anyway).
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    Bostrom is a philosopher, and goes into some of the philosophical details and strategies of a way of ensuring that an AI won't do something stupid (from the human perspective) --  perhaps, a future-proof version of Asimov's
                    <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Three_Laws_of_Robotics" rel="nofollow">
                     Three Laws of Robotics
                    </a>
                    or some way to express "What Would a Human Do". In any case, pitfalls all around, let alone the task of programming such rules.
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    So, a book rich in ideas and a good overview of the field. However, in my opinion, the writer often went beyond the main point he was trying to make, and spend half a page on something that is only tangentially related to the question at hand. Anyway, well worth reading.
                   </span>
                   <a data-text-id="16794140598174079797" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">
                    ...more
                   </a>
                  </span>
                 </div>

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_22928874'), "\n\n  &lt;h2&gt;&lt;a class=\"readable\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22928874-rise-of-the-robots?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\"&gt;Rise of the Robots: Technology and the Threat of a Jobless Future&lt;\/a&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\n\n      &lt;div&gt;\n        by &lt;a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/838237.Martin_Ford?a=7&amp;origin=related_works\"&gt;Martin Ford&lt;\/a&gt;\n      &lt;\/div&gt;\n\n          &lt;div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\"&gt;\n            &lt;span class=\"minirating\"&gt;&lt;span class=\"stars staticStars\"&gt;&lt;a class=\"staticStar p10\" size=\"12x12\" title=\"4.07 of 5 stars\"&gt;4.07 of 5 stars&lt;\/a&gt;&lt;a class=\"staticStar p10\" size=\"12x12\" title=\"4.07 of 5 stars\"&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;&lt;a class=\"staticStar p10\" size=\"12x12\" title=\"4.07 of 5 stars\"&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;&lt;a class=\"staticStar p10\" size=\"12x12\" title=\"4.07 of 5 stars\"&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;&lt;a class=\"staticStar p3\" size=\"12x12\" title=\"4.07 of 5 stars\"&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;&lt;\/span&gt; 4.07 avg rating &mdash; 133 ratings&lt;\/span&gt;            &mdash; published 2015\n          &lt;\/div&gt;\n\n    &lt;div class=\"addBookTipDescription\"&gt;\n      \n&lt;span id=\"freeTextContainer17788912923917177683\"&gt; A New York Times Science BestsellerWhat are the jobs of the future? How many will there be? And who will have them? We might imagineand hopethat todays industrial revolution will unfold like the last: even as some jobs are eliminated, more will b&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;span id=\"freeText17788912923917177683\" style=\"display:none\"&gt; A New York Times Science BestsellerWhat are the jobs of the future? How many will there be? And who will have them? We might imagineand hopethat todays industrial revolution will unfold like the last: even as some jobs are eliminated, more will be created to deal with the new innovations of a new era. In Rise of the Robots, Silicon Valley entrepreneur Martin Ford argues that this is absolutely not the case. As technology continues to accelerate and machines begin taking care of themselves, fewer people will be necessary. Artificial intelligence is already well on its way to making good jobs obsolete: many paralegals, journalists, office workers, and even computer programmers are poised to be replaced by robots and smart software. As progress continues, blue and white collar jobs alike will evaporate, squeezing working- and middle-class families ever further. At the same time, households are under assault from exploding costs, especially from the two major industrieseducation and health carethat, so far, have not been transformed by information technology. The result could well be massive unemployment and inequality as well as the implosion of the consumer economy itself.In Rise of the Robots, Ford details what machine intelligence and robotics can accomplish, and implores employers, scholars, and policy makers alike to face the implications. The past solutions to technological disruption, especially more training and education, arent going to work, and we must decide, now, whether the future will see broad-based prosperity or catastrophic levels of inequality and economic insecurity. Rise of the Robots is essential reading for anyone who wants to understand what accelerating technology means for their own economic prospectsnot to mention those of their childrenas well as for This book investigates what cannot be known. Rather than exploring the amazing facts that science, mathematics, and reason have revealed to us, this work studies what science, mathematics, and reason tell us cannot be revealed. In &quot;The Outer Limits of Reason,&quot; Noson Yanofsky considers what cannot be predicted, described, or known, and what will never be understood. He discusses the limitations of computers, physics, logic, and our own thought processes.\n\nYanofsky describes simple tasks that would take computers trillions of centuries to complete and other problems that computers can never solve; perfectly formed English sentences that make no sense; different levels of infinity; the bizarre world of the quantum; the relevance of relativity theory; the causes of chaos theory; math problems that cannot be solved by normal means; and statements that are true but cannot be proven. He explains the limitations of our intuitions about the world -- our ideas about space, time, and motion, and the complex relationship between the knower and the known.\n\nMoving from the concrete to the abstract, from problems of everyday language to straightforward philosophical questions to the formalities of physics and mathematics, Yanofsky demonstrates a myriad of unsolvable problems and paradoxes. Exploring the various limitations of our knowledge, he shows that many of these limitations have a similar pattern and that by investigating these patterns, we can better understand the structure and limitations of reason itself. Yanofsky even attempts to look beyond the borders of reason to see what, if anything, is out there.&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;a data-text-id=\"3093284297832145841\" href=\"#\" The Singularity is triggered by the tremendous growth of human and computing intelligence that is an almost inevitable outcome of Moore\'s Law. Since the book\'s publication, the coming of the Singularity is now eagerly anticipated by many of the leading thinkers in Silicon Valley, from PayPal mastermind Peter Thiel to Google co-founder Larry Page. The formation of the Singularity University, and the huge popularity of the Singularity website kurzweilai.com, speak to the importance of this intellectual movement.But what about the average person? How will the Singularity affect our daily livesour jobs, our families, and our wealth?Singularity Rising: Surviving and Thriving in a Smarter, Richer, and More Dangerous World focuses on the implications of a future society faced with an abundance of human and artificial intelligence. James D. Miller, an economics professor and popular speaker on the Singularity, reveals how natural selection has been increasing human intelligence over the past few thousand years and speculates on how intelligence enhancements will shape civilization over the next forty years.Miller considers several possible scenarios in this coming singularity:-A merger of man and machine making society fantastically wealthy and nearly immortal-Competition with billions of cheap AIs drive human wages to almost nothing while making investors rich-Businesses rethink investment decisions to take into account an expected future period of intense creative destruction-Inequality drops worldwide as technologies mitigate the cognitive cost of living in impoverished environments-Drugs designed to fight Alzheimer\'s disease and keep soldiers alert on battlefields have the fortunate side effect of increasing all of their users IQs, which, in turn, adds a percentage points to worldwide economic growthSingularity Rising offers predictions about the economic implications for a future of widely expanding intelligence and practical career and investment advice on flourishing on the way to the It has a wealth of new and exciting ideas, and will help shake up our notions regarding the age, sex, color, and topic clichs of the current public perception of science.Steven Pinker, author of The Humans steer the future not because we\'re the stronge&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;span id=\"freeText287112926699849196\" style=\"display:none\"&gt;What happens when machines become smarter than humans? Forget lumbering Terminators. The power of an artificial intelligence (AI) comes from its intelligence, not physical strength and laser guns. Humans steer the future not because we\'re the strongest or the fastest but because we\'re the smartest. When machines become smarter than humans, we\'ll be handing them the steering wheel. What promisesand perilswill these powerful machines present? Stuart Armstrongs new book navigates these questions with clarity and wit. Can we instruct AIs to steer the future as we desire? What goals should we program into them? It turns out this question is difficult to answer! Philosophers have tried for thousands of years to define an ideal world, but there remains no consensus. The prospect of goal-driven, smarter-than-human AI gives moral philosophy a new urgency. The future could be filled with joy, art, compassion, and beings living worthwhile and wonderful livesbut only if were able to precisely define what a &quot;good&quot; world is, and skilled enough to describe it perfectly to a computer program. AIs, like computers, will do what we saywhich is not necessarily what we mean. Such precision requires encoding the entire system of human values for an AI: explaining them to a mind that is alien to us, defining every ambiguous term, clarifying every edge case. Moreover, our values are fragile: in some cases, if we mis-define a single piece of the puzzlesay, consciousnesswe end up with roughly 0% of the value we intended to reap, instead of 99% of the value. Though an understanding of the problem is only beginning to spread, researchers from fields ranging from philosophy to computer science to economics are working together to conceive and test solutions. Are we up to the challenge? A mathematician by training, Armstrong is a Research Fellow at the Future of Humanity Institute (FHI) at Oxford University. His research focuses on formal decision theory, the risks and possibilities of AI, the long term potential for intelligent life (and the difficulties of predicting this), and anthropic (self-locating) probability. Armstrong wrote Smarter Than Us at the request of the Machine Intelligence Research Institute, a non-profit organization studying the theoretical underpinnings of artificial        <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor">
        <div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer">
         <h2 class="brownBackground">
          <a href="/author/show/608087.Nick_Bostrom">
           About Nick Bostrom
          </a>
         </h2>
        </div>
        <div class="bigBoxBody">
         <div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <a href="/author/show/608087.Nick_Bostrom">
           <img alt="608087" class="right" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1408029377p2/608087.jpg"/>
          </a>
          <div class="readable">
           <span id="freeTextContainer17801604704646617883">
            Nick Bostrom (born Niklas Bostrm on 10 March 1973) is a Swedish philosopher at the University of Oxford known for his work on existential risk and the anthropic principle. He holds a PhD from the London School of Economics (2000). He is currently the director of both The Future of Humanity Institute and the Oxford Martin Programme on the Impacts of Future Technology as part of the Oxford Martin S
           </span>
           <span id="freeText17801604704646617883" style="display:none">
            Nick Bostrom (born Niklas Bostrm on 10 March 1973) is a Swedish philosopher at the University of Oxford known for his work on existential risk and the anthropic principle. He holds a PhD from the London School of Economics (2000). He is currently the director of both The Future of Humanity Institute and the Oxford Martin Programme on the Impacts of Future Technology as part of the Oxford Martin School at Oxford University.
            <br/>
            <br/>
            He is the author of over 200 publications, including Superintelligence: Paths, Dangers, Strategies and Anthropic Bias. He has been awarded the Eugene R. Gannon Award and has been listed in Foreign Policy's Top 100 Global Thinkers list.
            <br/>
            <br/>
            In addition to his writing for academic and popular press, Bostrom makes frequent media appearances in which he talks about transhumanism-related topics such as artificial intelligence, superintelligence, mind uploading, cryonics, nanotechnology, human enhancement, and the simulation argument.
            <br/>
            <br/>
            Bostrom was born in 1973 in Helsingborg, Sweden.
            <br/>
            <br/>
            Bostrom is favourable towards "human enhancement", or "self-improvement and human perfectibility through the ethical application of science", as well as a critic of bio-conservative views. He has proposed the reversal test for reducing status quo bias in bioethical discussions of human enhancement.
            <br/>
            <br/>
            In 1998, Bostrom co-founded (with
            <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/author/show/301209.David_Pearce" title="David Pearce">
             David Pearce
            </a>
            ) the World Transhumanist Association (which has since changed its name to Humanity+). In 2004, he co-founded (with James Hughes) the Institute for Ethics and Emerging Technologies. In 2005 he was appointed Director of the newly created Future of Humanity Institute in Oxford. Bostrom is the 2009 recipient of the Eugene R. Gannon Award for the Continued Pursuit of Human Advancement and was named in Foreign Policy's 2009 list of top global thinkers "for accepting no limits on human potential."
            <br/>
            <br/>
            Bostrom has addressed the philosophical question of humanity's long-term survival. He defines an existential risk as one in which an "adverse outcome would either annihilate Earth-originating intelligent life or permanently and drastically curtail its potential." In the 2008 volume "Global Catastrophic Risks", editors Bostrom and Cirkovic offer a detailed taxonomy of existential risk, and various papers link existential risk to observer selection effects and the Fermi paradox.
            <br/>
            <br/>
            Bostrom has published numerous articles on anthropic reasoning, as well as the book Anthropic Bias: Observation Selection Effects in Science and Philosophy. In the book, he criticizes previous formulations of the anthropic principle, including those of
            <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6050972.Brandon_Carter" title="Brandon Carter">
             Brandon Carter
            </a>
            ,
            <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5268945.John_Leslie" title="John Leslie">
             John Leslie
            </a>
            ,
            <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/author/show/113384.John_Barrow" title="John Barrow">
             John Barrow
            </a>
            , and
            <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/author/show/61625.Frank_Tipler" title="Frank Tipler">
             Frank Tipler
            </a>
            .
            <br/>
            <br/>
            Bostrom appeared a 2003 episode of Horizon. More recently, he's appeared in two episodes of Closer to Truth hosted by Robert Lawrence Kuhn, once to discuss the universe and once to discuss his agnosticism.
            <br/>
            <br/>
            More:
            <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nick_Bostrom" rel="nofollow" target="_blank">
             http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nick_Bos...
            </a>
           </span>
           <a data-text-id="17801604704646617883" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">
            ...more
           </a>
          </div>
          <a class="actionLink moreLink" href="/author/show/608087.Nick_Bostrom">

      &lt;div class=" clearFloats bigBox"&gt;&lt;div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"&gt;&lt;h2 class="brownBackground"&gt;&lt;a href="/trivia/work/37286000-superintelligence-paths-dangers-strategies"&gt;Trivia About Superintelligence...&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/h2&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class="bigBoxBody"&gt;&lt;div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent"&gt;
				&lt;p class="mediumText"&gt;No trivia or quizzes yet. &lt;a href="/trivia/work/37286000-superintelligence-paths-dangers-strategies"&gt;Add some now &raquo;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;div class="clear"&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class="bigBoxBottom"&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;

    &lt;div class=" clearFloats bigBox"&gt;&lt;div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"&gt;&lt;h2 class="brownBackground"&gt;&lt;a href="/work/quotes/37286000-superintelligence-paths-dangers-strategies"&gt;Quotes from Superintelligence: Pat...&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/h2&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class="bigBoxBody"&gt;&lt;div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent"&gt;
        &lt;div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px"&gt;
          &lt;span class="readable"&gt;&ldquo;Far from being the smartest possible biological species, we are probably better thought of as the stupidest possible biological species capable of starting a technological civilization - a niche we filled because we got there first, not because we are in any sense optimally adapted to it.&rdquo;&lt;/span&gt;
          &lt;nobr&gt;
            &mdash;
            &lt;a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/1330609-far-from-being-the-smartest-possible-biological-species-we-are"&gt;9 likes&lt;/a&gt;
          &lt;/nobr&gt;
          &lt;div class="clear"&gt;&lt;/div&gt;
        &lt;/div&gt;
        &lt;div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px"&gt;
          &lt;span class="readable"&gt;&ldquo;Let an ultraintelligent machine be defined as a machine that can far surpass all the intellectual activities of any man however clever. Since the design of machines is one of these intellectual activities, an ultraintelligent machine could design even better machines; there would then unquestionably be an intelligence explosion, and the intelligence of man would be left far behind. Thus the first ultraintelligent machine is the last invention that man need ever make, provided that the machine is docile enough to tell us how to keep it under control.&rdquo;&lt;/span&gt;
          &lt;nobr&gt;
            &mdash;
            &lt;a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/6948822-let-an-ultraintelligent-machine-be-defined-as-a-machine-that"&gt;8 likes&lt;/a&gt;
          &lt;/nobr&gt;
          &lt;div class="clear"&gt;&lt;/div&gt;
        &lt;/div&gt;
      &lt;a class="actionLink" href="/work/quotes/37286000-superintelligence-paths-dangers-strategies" style="float: right"&gt;More quotes&hellip;&lt;/a&gt;
&lt;div class="clear"&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class="bigBoxBottom"&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;
